from pptx.enum.shapes import PP_PLACEHOLDER

titles = []
for i, slide in enumerate(prs.slides, start=1):
    title_text = None
    for shape in slide.shapes:
        if shape.is_placeholder and shape.placeholder_format.type == PP_PLACEHOLDER.TITLE:
            title_text = shape.text.strip()
            break
    titles.append((i, title_text))
titles

slide_summaries = []
for i, slide in enumerate(prs.slides, start=1):
    lines = []
    for shape in slide.shapes:
        if hasattr(shape,"text"):
            txt = shape.text.strip()
            if txt:
                # split into lines
                for line in txt.splitlines():
                    line = line.strip()
                    if line:
                        lines.append(line)
    first = lines[0] if lines else ""
    slide_summaries.append((i, first, lines[:8]))
slide_summaries

def print_slide(i):
    slide = prs.slides[i-1]
    print("SLIDE", i)
    for shape in slide.shapes:
        if hasattr(shape,"text"):
            txt = shape.text
            if txt.strip():
                print("BLOCK:")
                print(txt.strip())
    print("\n" + "="*40 + "\n")

for i in range(1, 10):
    print_slide(i)

- You want this deck judged specifically against the Series A expectations in the MOBILE F2P INVESTOR PITCH DECK GUIDE, not seed.  
- We are not inventing any live metrics. Where the guide expects real data and you do not yet have it, I will call that out explicitly rather than faking Series A readiness.  
- The 14–slide skeleton from the guide is the reference structure, even though your deck currently runs at ~26 slides.

Plan  
1. Map the current Sarge deck to the guide’s S01–S14 Series A skeleton.

## 2. Cluster by cluster critique for Series A

I will focus on “does this match Series A expectations” rather than “is this good Seed”.

### Cluster A: Story and fantasy (S01–S03)

Guide expectations  

- S01: Cover and one line summary now include live or soft launch status and any headline metrics, all backed by S08 data.fileciteturn2file11  
- S02: Vision pitch is 2–4 sentences, reflecting what actually exists and the bigger franchise ambition.  
- S03: Player problem and fantasy are grounded in real behaviour and feedback, with at least one quantified link, for example session length, fairness perception, or play patterns.fileciteturn2file11  

What you have

- Cover slide clearly states concept and Telegram focus, plus contact. Good clarity of product description and platform choice.fileciteturn2file8  
- Loop slide (3) gives a fast fantasy of chat to raid to tournaments.  
- Initial playable slide (4) talks about testing, content runway and shows that key systems exist.fileciteturn2file8  
- There is no explicit “player problem and fantasy” slide with real player quotes or behaviour, and no link from behaviour to fantasy.  

Series A gaps / issues  

- S01 is not framed as a live or soft launched product. The deck still reads as “prototype in testing” rather than “operating game with cohort data”. That is correct for your stage, but it conflicts with the Series A template.  
- No concise S02 “vision and elevator” slide that reflects current reality plus long-term ambition. You are forcing investors to infer this from several slides.  
- No real S03. For Series A, you want to show actual evidence that players care about fairness, depth, session length, tournaments etc, pulled from Discord quotes, surveys, or telemetry.  

Conclusion: Story is strong for early Seed. It is not yet Series A style because it is not anchored in real player evidence.

---

### Cluster B: Product, core loop and live ops (S04, S05, S10)

Guide expectations  

- S04: Show what is live today (modes, regions, platforms), what changed since Seed, and which product risks are de-risked.fileciteturn2file11  
- S05: Core loop slide explains how the loop actually behaves, with progression distributions and at least one “we changed X and saw Y in cohorts” example.fileciteturn2file11  
- S10: Live ops slide summarises events/seasons already run, learnings, and a 12–18 month live ops roadmap aligned with metrics and model.fileciteturn2file11  

What you have  

- Product status and loop are well described, but all framed as current build and planned content runway, not as “live product with history”.fileciteturn2file8  
- No “before vs after” examples of design changes and impact on retention, engagement, or monetisation.  
- Roadmap slide is mostly about KPI gates and cash tranches, not about live ops cadence, season types, event learning, or how these tie back into KPIs.fileciteturn2file6turn2file9  

Series A gaps / issues  

- No explicit list of which product risks have been de-risked by live usage at meaningful scale.  
- No progression distributions, elder game view, or example of a design change that moved curves.  
- S10 content is roadmap plus capital discipline. For Series A, you would also want history of live ops experiments and their outcomes.  

Conclusion: Product slides are well thought through as design and plan. They are not yet Series A, because there is no history of changes grounded in live data.

Guide expectations  

- S06: Market and audience slide combines segmentation with actual early penetration and regional learnings.fileciteturn2file11

- A headline “100M players in 100 days / 1.8B year 1 / 4M daily revenue” taken from Telegram hit examples, but not clearly labelled as referencing other titles.fileciteturn2file1  

Series A gaps / issues  

- Market sizing is not structured by segments (for example Telegram mid-core strategy in top regions) with any sense of attainable share.  
- No quantified penetration of your own alpha/waitlist into target segments beyond the one Discord stat.fileciteturn2file10  
- Competitive slide is more inspiration than head-to-head. There is no grid comparing Sarge to actual Telegram competitors and relevant off-platform strategy titles.  

Conclusion: The “Why Telegram” narrative is strong. For Series A, this cluster is missing your own position and penetration within that market.

### Cluster D: Data, monetisation, UA, measurement (S08, S09, S11)

This is the heart of a Series A deck. The guide is explicit that by Series A this cluster becomes the core of the story.fileciteturn2file11  

Guide expectations  

- S08: Traction and validation shows several months of MAU/DAU, DAU/MAU, sessions, time per DAU, and cohort retention curves with commentary.  
- S09: Monetisation slide shows actual ARPDAU, payer rate, ARPPU, revenue mix, early LTV ranges, and impact of design changes.fileciteturn2file11

What you have

- A “Traction & 30-Day Validation Plan” that is actually a test plan: hypotheses, metric targets, methods, windows, and owners.fileciteturn2file6turn2file11  
- UA channel mix, gate rules, and weekly test cadence are well defined conceptually, but almost entirely pre-launch.fileciteturn2file10turn2file12  

Series A gaps / issues  

- There is no S08-style slide showing current MAU/DAU, retention curves, or time per DAU by cohort.  
- There is no S09-style slide showing real ARPDAU, payer rate distribution, revenue mix, or evidence that design changes moved monetisation.  
- There is no S11-style slide showing actual spend by channel and corresponding CPIs / payback.  
- The “Unit economics snapshot” and “Assumptions and Gates” slides are excellent *for a pre-launch or Seed deck* but do not meet the “live cohorts and payback curves” expectation of Series A.fileciteturn2file10turn2file11  

Conclusion: This deck is structurally ready for data, but currently it is a Seed-style hypothesis and validation plan, not a Series A data cluster.

## 3. Summary: what needs doing for Series A

High-level reality check first  

Per your own guide, a true Series A deck assumes:

- Live or advanced soft launch game with cohorts over several months.  
- Real KPIs for retention, ARPDAU, payer rate, CPI, and payback.fileciteturn2file11  

This deck currently presents:

- Prototype / early playable status and a validation plan.fileciteturn2file8turn2file6  
- Forecast metrics and test gates, not live cohorts and payback curves.fileciteturn2file6turn2file10turn2file11  

So the honest position is:

- Structurally you are ahead of a typical Seed deck.  
- Evidence level is still pre-Series A.  
- You can upgrade structure and language now, but you cannot meet the full Series A evidence bar until you have cohorts.

With that in mind, here is the worklist.

### Priority 1: Data and traction cluster

1. Add a new S08 “Traction and metrics overview” slide that will eventually show MAU/DAU, retention curves, ARPDAU, payer rate, CPI ranges, and payback in one place. For now, this can hold whatever real signals you have (waitlist, early test cohorts) plus clearly labelled gaps.  
2. Reframe “Traction & 30-Day Validation Plan” as a sub-slide in the UA/experiments cluster, clearly labelled as “Validation Plan” rather than “Traction”.fileciteturn2file6turn2file11  
3. Make sure all forecast numbers are grouped and clearly labelled as forecasts, not mixed into “traction”.  

### Priority 2: Story and product alignment with reality

4. Update S01–S03 to explicitly describe current stage and constraints, with one clean cover, one elevator pitch slide, and one player problem/fantasy slide using real quotes and behavioural data where you have it.  
5. On product slides, add a simple “current state” block and a “de-risked vs remaining risks” block so investors see what is already solved versus open.  

### Priority 3: Market and competition

6. Replace or augment the general Hamster/Notcoin narrative with a structured “market and audience” slide that shows: target segment, Telegram market size, and your current penetration plus a simple competitive grid.fileciteturn2file1turn2file8turn2file11  

### Priority 4: UA and live ops detail

7. Add a dedicated S11-style UA and channel mix slide showing how you intend to use Telegram Ads, creators, and other channels, with a few concrete creative examples and test design. Later you will replace much of this with actual data.fileciteturn2file10turn2file11  
8. Upgrade the roadmap slide into a true S10 “live ops and roadmap” slide, separating event cadence, content beats, and their expected impact on KPIs from pure capital gating.fileciteturn2file6turn2file11  

### Priority 5: Org, team, and plan

9. Consolidate the team content into a single S12 slide plus a simple org snapshot, remove duplicated team slide variants, and explicitly show owners for product, live ops, data, and UA.fileciteturn2file2turn2file7turn2file9turn2file11  
10. Tune the plan / use of funds slides to make the scenario logic clearer (base/upside/downside, how you adjust spend if gates are missed) and add a clean closing slide with contact and data room pointers.fileciteturn2file2turn2file4turn2file5turn2file11  

---

## 4. First concrete fix: S08 “Traction and metrics overview” slide

Given your guide says “data slides are now the core of the Series A deck” and recommends a detailed metrics overview slide, I am starting here.fileciteturn2file11  

### 4.1 Intent of the slide

- Become the single place where a Series A investor can see the health of the game at a glance.  
- Cleanly separate what is real today from what is forecast.  
- Align with the model and the validation plan slides so there are no contradictions.

### 4.2 Proposed structure and content

**Slide title**  
`Traction and Metrics Overview`

**Top line band (headline metrics)**  

A simple three-column layout, labels only where you do not yet have numbers:

- Column 1: Usage  
  - `MAU: [X]`  
  - `DAU: [Y]`  
  - `DAU / MAU: [Z %]`  
  - `Avg sessions per DAU: [A]`  
  - `Avg minutes per DAU: [B]`  

- Column 2: Retention  
  - `Key cohort: [Country / release wave / channel]`  
  - `D1 / D7 / D30: [.. / .. / .. %]`  
  - `D60 / D90 (if available): [.. / .. %]`  

- Column 3: Monetisation and UA  
  - `Payer rate: [.. %]`  
  - `ARPDAU: £[..]`  
  - `ARPPU: £[..]`  
  - `CPI range: £[low] – £[high]`  
  - `Payback: [.. days] in [main channel]`  

Where you do not yet have live figures, you can either leave as `[TBD after first cohorts]` or write `Not yet launched: currently validating via plan on slide [19]`.

**Middle band (cohort view)**  

Left half:

- Small line chart: “Retention by cohort”  
  - X-axis: Days since install (0–90)  
  - Y-axis: Retention percent  
  - 2–3 labelled cohorts, for example:  
    - `Alpha cohort – UK early adopters`  
    - `Soft launch wave 1 – Tier 2 geos`  
    - `Creator test cohort – Telegram Ads`  

Right half:  

Short bullet commentary, for example templated as:

- `Alpha cohorts showed D7 of [.. %]; rebuild of onboarding in [month] improved D7 to [.. %].`  
- `Weekend events increased D30 by [.. points] for cohorts who engaged at least once.`  
- `Creator-led cohorts have lower CPI but slightly lower D30; we are testing a different early quest structure for them.`  

You will fill this once you have data. For now, if you have zero cohort curves, this section can honestly say:

- `Live cohorts pending: first 3 test cohorts scheduled in [month]. See validation plan slide for exact dates and metrics.`

**Bottom band (alignment note)**  

A small text box at the bottom linking this slide to the rest of the deck and model:

## 1. Why a rebuild is the better move

Given your constraints:

- You are already at 26 slides.  
- There is duplication and some mislabelling (for example “Traction” that is really a test plan, team content in two places, multiple “Why Telegram” passes).  
- The guide’s Series A skeleton expects around 14 core slides, plus a very small number of “must have” extras.

Trying to “fix” this deck slide by slide will:

- Keep accreting content and complexity.  
- Make it hard to maintain a clean story arc.  
- Make slide count very hard to bring down.

A rebuild, anchored to the guide, lets us:

- Start from the Series A skeleton.  
- Decide what *must* be in the core 14, and move the rest to appendix or remove.  
- Copy and compress the best existing content into the new slots.

You do not lose the work. You reorganise it.

from pptx import Presentation
prs = Presentation(deck_path)
len(prs.slides)

[ASSISTANT]
for i, slide in enumerate(prs.slides):
    title = slide.shapes.title.text if slide.shapes.title else ""
    print(i+1, "TITLE:", repr(title))
    # print first textbox content besides title
    texts = []
    for shape in slide.shapes:
        if not shape.has_text_frame: 
            continue
        if shape == slide.shapes.title:
            continue
        txt = shape.text.strip()
        if txt:
            texts.append(txt.replace("\n", " | "))
    if texts:
        print("   BODY:", " || ".join(texts[:2]))
    print()

[ASSISTANT]
def slide_text(slide):
    parts=[]
    for shape in slide.shapes:
        if shape.has_text_frame:
            parts.append(shape.text)
    return "\n---\n".join(parts)

for i in range(1, 11):
    print("SLIDE", i)
    print(slide_text(prs.slides[i-1])[:1200])
    print("========\n")

    print(slide_text(prs.slides[idx-1]))
    print("====\n")

## S09. Business model and fairness

**Purpose**  
Explain how you make money and how you protect fairness, in one place.

**Suggested title**  
Business model and fairness

**Body**  

- Monetisation pillars  
  - Cosmetics and battle passes fund the game.  
  - No purchasable stats, units, timers or raw power.  
  - Store offers cosmetics, effects, skins and UI themes only.  
- Fairness guardrails  
  - Matchmaking uses MMR and recency only.  
  - Payer vs non-payer win rate gap under 1 percentage point at matched MMR.  
  - Correlation of spend to win rate within MMR bands r ≤ 0.05.  
  - At least 90 percent of revenue from non-power cosmetics.  
  - Top 1 percent of spenders at or below 35 percent of revenue.  
- Link to retention and ARPDAU  
  - Quests, limited time events and creator-run tournaments give players reasons to return and to spend on identity and celebration.  
  - We trade short-term “whale squeezing” for long-term retention and broad payer participation.

**Sources to reuse**  

- Your current “Business model and fairness policy” slide.

**Guide alignment**  

- Strong alignment to S09 on design philosophy and revenue shape.  
- Series A expectation of real ARPDAU, payer rate and revenue mix data will come later. For now, this is policy plus targets, not observed metrics.

## Where this deviates from the guide and why

- Series A data expectations  
  - The guide assumes you can show several months of cohort-based retention, monetisation and UA data in S08, S09 and S11.  
  - In this rebuild, those slides are structured exactly as the guide outlines, but populated with targets, policies and plans until you have real cohorts.  
  - This is the main unavoidable deviation.  
- Stage labelling  
  - The guide often writes as if “Series A” implies soft launch or live game. You are earlier than that, so we explicitly label stage on S01 and S08 instead of stretching language.  
- Market sizing detail  
  - The guide leans towards a more formal TAM/SAM/SOM breakdown by Series A. Here, we outline the logic and leave room for you to plug in numbers once you are comfortable with a specific sizing model.

Everything else is structurally aligned: 14 slide skeleton, story slots, product and market, data cluster, organisation and plan.

  

## Self-audit

- Coverage: All 14 core slides are specified with purpose, titles and concrete bullet content, plus a compact appendix.  
- Alignment: Structure matches the guide’s Series A skeleton and emphasis. Main misalignment is evidence level, which is called out explicitly.  
- Reuse: Fairness policy, Telegram positioning, UA gates, risk table, hiring plan and financial scenarios are all reused conceptually from the existing deck. Wording is tightened rather than invented from scratch.  
- Risk: The biggest risk is over-promising Series A readiness without live metrics. The rebuild avoids that by clearly framing metrics as targets and plans until cohorts exist.

S05. Core Loop and Progression  

**Title**  
Core loop and progression  

**Section: Core loop**  
- Tap a link in a Telegram chat to open Sarge in under 5 seconds  
- Deploy and command units in real-time battles  
- Earn resources and rewards based on performance  
- Invest resources into base upgrades, tech tree and army composition  
- Unlock new units, abilities and tournament brackets  

**Section: Progression and elder game**  
- Early game  
  - Learn core mechanics, unlock first units and basic upgrades  
  - Short, snackable sessions that match Telegram use patterns  
- Mid game  
  - Deeper tech choices, specialisations and meaningful counters  
  - Alliances and social structures begin to matter  
- Elder game  
  - Territory control, alliance coordination and sustained wars  
  - High-stakes creator-run tournaments with unique formats and rewards  

**Section: Design goals**  
- Multiple viable strategies rather than a single dominant meta  
- Clear, readable battles that still reward mastery and long-term planning  
- Progression built for years, not months

S06. Market and Why Telegram  

**Title**  
Market and why Telegram  

**Section: Telegram as a platform**  
- 900M plus monthly active users, with Mini Apps that run inside chat threads  
- Instant launch from links in chats or channels, no app store visit or install  
- Viral mechanics built into the platform: forwards, group chats, channels and bots  

**Section: Strategy gap on Telegram**  
- Current top performers are clicker-style tap games and meme-driven titles  
- Almost no deep, fair, session-friendly mid-core strategy built for Telegram from day one  
- Sarge targets a wedge of players who already consume strategy content and follow gaming creators on Telegram, but cannot yet play a true war game there  

**Section: Opportunity (high level)**  
- If we capture a low single-digit share of Telegram users who play strategy titles today, Sarge can support multi-million DAU  
- Early hits like Notcoin and Hamster Kombat demonstrate how quickly a well-designed Telegram game can scale when it matches the platform  

**Section: What Sarge unlocks**  
- A flagship strategy franchise designed for Telegram’s rails  
- A new class of creator-owned tournaments and events inside chat

S07. Competition and Position  

**Title**  
Competition and position  

**Section: Comparison grid (in text)**  

Depth and skill  
- Sarge Universe  
  - Full army building, clear counters, and long-term elder-game strategy  
- Telegram tap games  
  - Simple counters, shallow progression, often single dominant path  
- Classic mobile 4X / war  
  - Deep but grind-heavy, often chores and time gates  

Friction and access  
- Sarge Universe  
  - Open from a link in chat, straight into battle, no install  
- Telegram tap games  
  - Also instant, but limited to very simple mechanics  
- Classic mobile 4X / war  
  - Store page, download, install, long tutorial before first real fight  

Fairness and monetisation  
- Sarge Universe  
  - No purchasable stats, units or timers  
  - Cosmetics-first economy with clear guardrails on pay for power  
- Telegram tap games  
  - Often ad-driven and attention-driven, minimal fairness considerations  
- Classic mobile 4X / war  
  - Heavy use of time skips, purchasable power and aggressive VIP systems  

Social and creator upside  
- Sarge Universe  
  - Creator-run tournaments integrated into the game, with revenue sharing and real ownership of events  
- Telegram tap games  
  - Limited creator integration, mostly referral codes and basic promos  
- Classic mobile 4X / war  
  - Creators operate outside the game through streams and content, not as first-class organisers inside the game  

**Bottom line note**  
- Sarge combines Telegram’s instant access with deep strategy and fair monetisation, giving both players and creators something they cannot get from existing options

S09. Business Model and Fairness  

**Title**  
Business model and fairness  

**Section: Monetisation pillars**  
- Free to start and free to compete, with no pay-for-power  
- Cosmetics and presentation  
  - Skins, effects, banners and other identity items  
- Battle pass  
  - Seasonal progression with cosmetics, currency and quality of life items  
- Creator events  
  - Optional entry fees for special tournaments, with rewards that are cosmetic or status-driven  

**Section: Fairness guardrails**  
- No purchasable stats, units, timers or direct power  
- Matchmaking based on MMR, recency and other performance factors, not spend  
- Win rate gap between payers and non-payers within a narrow band at equivalent MMR  
- Revenue mix targets  
  - At least 90 percent of revenue from cosmetics and non-power content  
  - Top 1 percent of spenders at or below 35 percent of total revenue  

**Section: Design intent**  
- Players can spend to look different, feel different and celebrate, but not to crush others  
- We trade short-term monetisation spikes for long-term retention, trust and a broad payer base  

---

S10. Unit Economics and KPI Gates  

**Title**  
Unit economics and KPI gates  

**Section: Target payback profile**  
- Base case target  
  - LTV to CPI of at least 3:1 within 180 days  
  - Retention and ARPDAU curves tuned for sustainable, profitable UA  
- Scaling rule  
  - We scale UA only when cohorts meet retention and payback thresholds defined here  

**Section: LTV components (Base case)**  
- Retention curve  
  - Healthy D1, D7 and D30, with a long tail of engaged strategy players  
- Monetisation  
  - ARPDAU target band by geo and segment  
  - Payer rate and ARPPU assumptions grounded in mid-core strategy benchmarks  
- LTV construction  
  - Cohort-level forecasts, aligned with our 12-month model and P and L  

**Section: KPI gate table (example structure)**  
- Gate 1: Soft launch viability  
  - D1, D7, D30 retention above defined thresholds  
  - CPI in priority channels within target bands  
- Gate 2: Scale testing  
  - Early payback within 180 days in at least one primary channel  
  - Stable ARPDAU and payer rate for key cohorts  
- Gate 3: Pre-Series A check  
  - Consistent LTV to CPI above 3:1 across scaled cohorts  
  - Sufficient data to support Series A conversations on real performance  

**Section: Safety valve**  
- UA spend throttles automatically if metrics drop below gate thresholds, extending runway instead of forcing growth at any cost  

---

S11. UA and Growth Engine  

**Title**  
UA and growth engine  

**Section: Channels**  
- Telegram Ads  
  - Primary paid UA channel, focused on Mini App and bot entry points  
- Creators and communities  
  - Creator-run tournaments, sponsored events and content as quasi-UA  
  - Revenue sharing tied to tournament performance and player spend  
- Classic UA (Meta, Google and others)  
  - Tested only once LTV supports these channels at scale  

**Section: Creative and funnel**  
- Creative themes  
  - “Tap to raid in your chat” moments that show instant battles and big swings  
  - Squad invites, alliance calls to arms and tournament highlights  
- Funnel  
  - Deep links into squads, tournaments and replays rather than a generic menu  
  - Clear on-ramp to the first meaningful battle in seconds  

**Section: Experiment rhythm**  
- Weekly  
  - New creative variations per channel  
  - Cohort performance review and budget reallocation  
- Monthly  
  - Larger experiments on early game structure, onboarding and time to first win  
- Measurement  
  - All UA experiments measured at cohort level, aligned with the financial model and KPI gates  

**Section: Success rules**  
- CPI, retention and payback thresholds per channel  
- No “volume for its own sake”; we scale only when cohorts obey the rules set in S10

Appendix A1. Detailed Risks and Mitigations  

**Title**  
Risks and mitigations  

**Table structure (content)**  

For each risk, list: Description, Trigger, Mitigation, Owner  

Examples (you can expand):  

- Platform risk  
  - Telegram policy changes for Mini Apps, bots or payments  
  - Trigger: Announced policy or enforcement shift  
  - Mitigation: Close relationship with Telegram team, fallback platforms for web and mobile, legal review  
  - Owner: CEO  

- UA and CPI risk  
  - CPIs on Telegram Ads or other channels higher than modelled  
  - Trigger: Cohorts consistently above CPI targets at target quality  
  - Mitigation: Shift mix toward creator tournaments and organic, optimise creatives, explore new channels  
  - Owner: Growth lead  

- Monetisation risk  
  - Fairness guardrails make it harder to reach target ARPDAU  
  - Trigger: Cohorts meet retention targets but lag ARPDAU targets  
  - Mitigation: Add more cosmetics, events and status items without breaking power rules, refine battle pass and event rewards  
  - Owner: CPO  

- Live ops and content risk  
  - Content cadence too slow to support elder game  
  - Trigger: Decline in elder-game engagement, alliance activity and tournament participation  
  - Mitigation: Tooling for faster content production, templated events, clear live ops calendar  
  - Owner: Live ops lead  

Add further rows for tech, team and regulatory risks as needed.

Appendix A2. Detailed 30-Day Validation Plan  

**Title**  
30-day validation plan  

**Section: Objectives**  
- Validate that core loop and early game can achieve target D1, D7 and D30 retention  
- Validate initial CPI bands in Telegram Ads and creator-driven UA  
- Collect first monetisation signals without overfitting on early spend  

**Table: Hypotheses, metrics, methods, owners**  

Retention  
- Hypothesis: Improved onboarding and early missions will deliver D1 and D7 retention within Base target bands  
- Metrics: D1, D3, D7 retention, tutorial completion rate, first win rate  
- Method: A/B variants of onboarding and early mission structure across cohorts  
- Owner: Product and design  

Monetisation  
- Hypothesis: A light cosmetic store plus early battle pass preview will generate healthy ARPDAU without harming fairness perception  
- Metrics: ARPDAU, payer rate, early ARPPU, NPS or fairness survey scores  
- Method: Time-limited test of store and pass, followed by player surveys and cohort analysis  
- Owner: Product and data  

UA and CPI  
- Hypothesis: Telegram Ads and creator posts can hit CPI targets for early cohorts  
- Metrics: CPI by channel, click-through rate, install-to-battle conversion  
- Method: Small budget UA tests per channel, with strict caps and fast readouts  
- Owner: Growth and UA  

**Section: Success bars and next steps**  
- If retention and CPI meet targets  
  - Expand cohorts, test more creatives, and move toward Gate 2  
- If metrics miss targets  
  - Focus on product and onboarding changes first, keep spend minimal, re-test before scaling

You are right to ask for a hard critique here. I will treat what we just built as a real draft and judge it slide by slide against the guide’s Series A expectations.

I will use this structure:

- A. High level: how this draft matches or breaks the Series A section of the guide  
- B. Slide by slide (S01–S14)  
- C. Appendix slides A1–A4  
- D. Top priority fixes

I will be blunt about where this is not really Series A, and also where the deck itself (as prose) is weaker than it should be.

  

## A. High level: alignment versus the guide

Guide’s Series A “core expectations” for the deck:

- Same 14 slide skeleton as Seed, but upgraded in  
  - Metrics: cohorts, retention curves, real ARPDAU, LTV, CPI and payback  
  - UA: channel mix, creative learnings, attribution and payback  
  - Organisation: live ops capability, process, hiring and scenarios  
  - Financials: scenario ranges and sensitivities tied to metrics, not pure belief  

Where our draft is aligned:

- We do use the 14 slide skeleton and the same cluster boundaries (story, product, market, data, org, plan).  
- The logic of S08, S09, S10 and S11 is structurally correct for Series A: there is a central metrics slide, a monetisation slide, a unit economics slide and a UA slide.  
- We have a proper organisation slide (S12) and a clear plan/ask slide (S13).  
- We have a compact appendix that holds risks, validation plan, hires and financial snapshot.

Where it is fundamentally not Series A:

- All data in S08, S09, S10, S11 is either a placeholder or a target. There is no actual MAU, DAU, retention, ARPDAU, CPI, payback or channel mix.  
- There is no “continuous stream of tests and releases” with cohort-based learnings. The validation plan is still theoretical.  
- The financial snapshot is not grounded in real volatility or live cohort behaviour.

You asked earlier to note when it is not reasonable to follow the guide. Here:

- It is not reasonable to satisfy the guide’s Series A metrics expectations until you have soft launch cohorts.  
- It is reasonable to copy the structure now, but we must not pretend it is Series A evidence. The current draft is structurally Series A, substantively late Seed.

On top of that, many of the slides are too wordy for Series A investors. The guide explicitly says that by Series A story slides should be shorter and sharper, because investors will focus conversation time on the data cluster. You are still carrying more text than they will tolerate.

  

## B. Slide by slide critique (S01–S14)

### S01. Cover and stage

Strengths

- Stage label is honest: “Initial playable in closed testing, raising to reach Series A metrics.” That directly fixes the “are we actually Series A” confusion.  
- Title and subtitle clearly say “strategy game” and “on Telegram” with no ambiguity.  
- Short bullets about one tap and creator tournaments are on point.

Issues against the guide

- The guide suggests that at Series A you can, and should, include “Live in [regions] since [month]” and possibly one short metric hint. You cannot do that yet, so this remains a Seed-level S01.  
- Visual tone is not addressed here. The guide pushes for S01 to match the current art and live UX, not an old concept. Our text is neutral; the actual PPT has to be checked for that.  
- This slide is acceptable for a pre Series A raise, but an actual Series A investor will note the “closed testing” line and reframe the round mentally.

Verdict

- Structurally fine, honesty good.  
- Not truly Series A because it cannot show live footprint.

  

### S02. Vision and elevator

Strengths

- Three short paragraphs, clear and consistent.  
- Correctly separates “what Sarge is, now” from “what it becomes at scale.”  
- Matches the guide’s expectation of two to four sentences.

Issues

- The guide suggests that at Series A you might add a short line such as “already reaching [X] monthly players with [Y] minutes per day.” You cannot, so we stay generic.  
- The tone is more “Seed narrative” than “we have seen what works with players.” There is no reference to anything learned from real players or press.  
- Slightly wordy for an elevator slide; could be tightened into 2 paragraphs.

Verdict

- Formally aligned, content still belief-driven.  
- Once cohorts and early press exist, this slide should be rewritten to reflect what players actually respond to.

  

### S03. Player problem and fantasy

Strengths

- Clearly articulates a Telegram specific problem (clickers and meme games, no deep strategy).  
- Fantasy bullets are concrete and match your design pillars: instant access, creator events, fairness, and cross device depth.  
- The “future quotes” box is a good placeholder for the guide’s requirement to root this in player language.

Issues

- The guide wants this backed by actual behaviour and quotes by Series A. We still have zero data or voice of player here.  
- It is on the long side: multiple bullets under problem and fantasy. Investors will skim this. The guide would prefer one sharp problem and one sharp fantasy, not a mini essay.  
- There is no quantified link to behaviour, such as “our early community spends [X] minutes talking about fairness” or “Y percent of strategy players in our survey expressed [need].”

Verdict

- Conceptually aligned but still high on prose, low on evidence.  
- Needs trimming and supporting player data later.

  

### S04. Product today – status and scope

Strengths

- Explicitly lists what is implemented versus what is “near-term additions.” This matches the guide’s request to show de-risked items versus remaining risk.  
- Mentions progression spine, fairness enforcement and tournament framework. That plays nicely with your fairness and creator narratives.  
- Honest about being pre soft launch.

Issues

- The guide expects, by Series A, a view of “how the real game has evolved,” including major product changes and their impact on metrics. Our version is static: no changes, no history.  
- No indication of how many builds, how often you ship, or any example of “we changed X and saw Y.”  
- It reads like a design doc excerpt rather than a live product summary. Some detail will wash over investors.

Verdict

- Good for Seed or pre Series A.  
- For Series A you will need to add the history of releases and their effect.

  

### S05. Core loop and progression

Strengths

Issues

- The guide’s Series A upgrade asks for “how the loop actually behaves” and ideally progression distributions or examples of curve changes. We have none of that yet.

Verdict

### S07. Competition and position

Strengths

### S08. Traction and metrics overview

This is the most critical slide against the Series A section of the guide.

Our S08:

- Strong “Why now” and “What we are offering” phrasing, consistent with the rest of the story.  
- Clear list of what is in the data room (build, docs, model, risk).  
- Contact repeated at the end, which is good deck hygiene.

Issues

- The guide hints at connecting S14 to the data room and model very explicitly, for example referencing a “Metrics pack” or specific tabs. We stay generic.  
- Could use one short line about the process you prefer (for example, “we run first calls focused on X, then share build/model after NDA”). Not required, but helpful.

Verdict

- Aligned and usable.  
- Minor polish and specificity would bring it fully in line with the guide.

  

## C. Appendix A1–A4 critique

### A1. Risks and mitigations

Strengths

- Follows the guide pattern: risk, trigger, mitigation, owner.  
- Covers platform, UA, monetisation, live ops, with space for more.

Issues

- The guide prefers these to be grounded in actual events and learnings by Series A. Right now it is entirely hypothetical.  
- Could be more specific on Telegram specific risks (payment flows, bot policy shifts, regulatory angles).

Verdict

- Correct structure, currently abstract.  
- Will need an update once you have lived through a few incidents.

  

### A2. 30 day validation plan

Strengths

- Clear hypotheses, metrics, methods and owners across retention, monetisation and UA.  
- Explicit success bars and “if miss, do X” behaviour.  
- Strong alignment with the guide’s emphasis on structured testing.

Issues

- For Series A, this becomes more of an “ongoing experiment framework” than an initial 30 day plan. It is still written as if everything is in the future.  
- No link back to S08: the guide would want a sense of how many of these tests have already been run and what changed.

Verdict

- Excellent pre Series A planning appendix.  
- Needs to evolve into a “history and pipeline of tests” slide later.

  

### A3. First ten hires

Strengths

- Roles, timing and focus are clear.  
- Tied to the org snapshot in S12.

Issues

- The guide for Series A expects you to already have a core live ops team. If many of these hires are still future, it signals earlier stage. That might be acceptable, but it is a mismatch relative to “typical Series A.”  
- It might need a short statement of actual headcount today versus post raise.

Verdict

### A4. 12 month financial snapshot

Strengths

- Correct lines for a P and L snapshot.  
- Scenario note summarises Base, Upside, Downside.  
- Points to a full model in the data room.

Issues

- Needs some explicit linkage to KPI assumptions (for example, “Base assumes D30 of X and CPI of Y, as shown on S08/S10”).

Verdict

- Structurally aligned with the guide.  
- Needs real figures and explicit KPI linkage.

  

## D. Top priority issues to fix, in order

If you want this deck to be judged as “Series A style, honest about stage,” the biggest critiques against the guide are:

1. **Data cluster is plans and targets, not traction**  
   - S08, S09, S10, S11 collectively do not meet the Series A evidence bar.  
   - This is not solvable with editing; it is a stage reality.  
   - Short term, the best you can do is be extremely explicit on every slide about what is real, what is internal tests, and what is a target.

2. **Story and product slides are slightly over written for Series A**  
   - S02, S03, S04, S05 are more verbose than the guide recommends once investors primarily care about metrics.  
   - You should trim them by 20 to 30 percent without losing content, to respect investor attention.

3. **Market and competition need more numbers and names**  
   - S06 and S07 are conceptually strong but still lack basic TAM and comparator title references.  
   - Even a rough, conservative TAM statement and a list of 3 to 5 named competitors would push these closer to the guide’s expectations.

4. **UA slide lacks channel mix, CPI and creative learnings**  
   - S11 is entirely forward looking.  
   - As soon as you have any UA tests, you will want to convert those into a simple table or graph with CPI ranges and early quality signals.

5. **Plan and financials need locked numbers and explicitly tied scenarios**  
   - S13 and A4 will look unfinished until you put real percentages, ranges and KPI anchors into them.

If you want, the next move is for me to pick one cluster and rewrite that cluster to be tighter and more Series A friendly, keeping the honesty about stage. For example, we can take S02–S05 and cut them down while improving investor readability, then move to the data cluster when you are ready.

S01. Cover and Stage  

**Title**  
SARGE UNIVERSE  
The strategy game on Telegram  

**Subtitle**  
Deep, fair war inside your Telegram chats  

**Stage label (small ribbon or tag)**  
Stage: Initial playable in closed testing. Raising to reach Series A metrics on Telegram.  

**Tagline bullets**  
- One-tap from chat into real battles, no app store friction  
- Build bases, fight real opponents and join creator-run tournaments without leaving Telegram  

**Footer**  
- Studio logo  
- Contact: [Name], [Role], [Email], [Telegram handle]  

  

S02. Vision and Elevator  

**Title**  
Vision  

**Body**  

Sarge Universe is a deep, session-friendly war game that runs entirely inside Telegram. Players drop from links in chats straight into real battles, then grow persistent armies and bases over time.  

Today we have a working core loop on Telegram, an initial playable build and a disciplined plan to validate retention, monetisation and UA on this platform.  

At scale, Sarge becomes the reference strategy franchise on Telegram: fair by design, creator-powered, and capable of supporting millions of daily players and tournaments across regions.  

  

S03. Player Problem and Fantasy  

**Title**  
Player problem and fantasy  

**Problem**  
- Telegram is full of clickers, tap games and meme titles, but almost no true mid-core strategy with depth, fairness and long-term progression.  
- Strategy players bounce between heavy mobile 4X and PC, with friction between their social graph and the games they play.  
- Creators on Telegram have large, engaged audiences but no deep, integrated game experiences they can own and monetise.  

**Fantasy Sarge delivers**  
- “My army lives in my chats.” I tap a link and I am in a real fight within seconds.  
- I play in tournaments run by creators and community leaders I already follow, with visible ladders and real bragging rights.  
- I trust that money buys expression and convenience, not raw power. I can compete without paying, and still feel respected.  

**Future proof point**  
- Slot for 2–3 real player quotes from early cohorts / community once tests are live.  

  

S04. Product Today – Status and Scope  

**Title**  
Product today  

**Current playable**  
- Core loop from Telegram link into first battle is implemented and stable.  
- Base building, army composition and PvP skirmishes are functional in the current build.  
- We can already exercise progression, fairness rules and early elder-game dynamics.  

**Systems in place**  
- Progression spine  
  - Resources, upgrades and tech tree up to elder-game tiers.  
  - Multiple unit families and counters already implemented.  
- Fairness enforcement  
  - Matchmaking based on skill and recency, not spend.  
  - No purchasable stats, units or timers in the live design.  
- Tournament framework  
  - Creator-run event scaffolding (brackets, rewards, rulesets) integrated into the game.  

**Next steps before soft launch**  
- Tighten onboarding and early campaign to teach depth without overwhelming new players.  
- Layer first live events and seasonal structure on top of the loop.  
- Wire full telemetry and cohort tracking into our financial model and UA tooling.  

  

S05. Core Loop and Progression  

**Title**  
Core loop and progression  

**Core loop**  
- Tap a Sarge link in a chat or channel.  
- Drop into a real-time battle in under five seconds.  
- Earn resources and rewards based on performance.  
- Invest into base upgrades, tech tree and army composition.  
- Unlock new units, abilities and tournament brackets.  

**Progression arc**  
- Early game  
  - Learn basic commands, unlock first units and upgrades.  
  - Short, snackable sessions that fit Telegram usage.  
- Mid game  
  - Deeper tech choices, army specialisation and real counter-play.  
  - Alliances and social structures begin to matter.  
- Elder game  
  - Territory control, alliance coordination and long-running wars.  
  - High-stakes creator-run tournaments with unique formats and rewards.  

**Design goals**  
- Multiple viable strategies, not a single dominant meta.  
- Battles that are readable in seconds yet reward deep mastery.  
- Progression tuned for years of play, not months.  

  

S06. Market and Why Telegram  

**Title**  
Market and why Telegram  

**Telegram as a platform**  
- 900M plus monthly active users, with Mini Apps that run inside chat threads.  
- Removing store friction turns “see a link in chat” into instant play, not a multi-step install.  
- Virality is native: forwards, channels, groups and bot commands are built-in growth rails.  

**Strategy gap on Telegram**  
- Current top Telegram hits skew towards tap-to-earn, clickers and simple farming loops.  
- There is almost no deep, fair mid-core strategy built for Telegram first.  
- Players and creators who enjoy strategy content have nowhere on-platform to play a serious war game.  

**Opportunity**  
- If Sarge captures even a low single-digit share of Telegram users who play strategy titles today, it supports multi-million DAU.  
- Early Telegram hits show that well-designed games can scale to tens of millions of users in weeks when they align with the platform.  

**What Sarge unlocks**  
- A flagship strategy franchise designed for Telegram’s rails.  
- A new class of creator-owned tournaments and events inside chat.  

  

S07. Competition and Position  

**Title**  
Competition and position  

**Depth and skill**  
- Sarge Universe: Full army building, tech choices and long-term elder game; multiple viable metas.  
- Telegram tap games: Light progression and simple loops; usually one “best way” to play.  
- Classic mobile 4X / war: Deep but grind-heavy, with chores and time gates.  

**Friction and access**  
- Sarge Universe: Open from a link in chat, straight into battle, no install.  
- Telegram tap games: Also instant, but built around thin mechanics.  
- Classic 4X / war: App store page, download, install, long tutorial before the first meaningful fight.  

**Fairness and monetisation**  
- Sarge Universe: No purchasable stats, units or timers; cosmetics-first monetisation with explicit guardrails.  
- Telegram tap games: Often ad-driven or tap-to-earn, minimal fairness consideration.  
- Classic 4X / war: Heavy use of time skips, purchasable power and aggressive VIP ladders.  

**Social and creator upside**  
- Sarge Universe: Creator-run tournaments with revenue sharing and in-game tools for hosting events.  
- Telegram tap games: Limited creator integration, mostly referral codes and promo.  
- Classic 4X / war: Creators operate outside the game via streams and content, not as in-game organisers.  

**Positioning sentence**  
- Sarge combines Telegram’s instant rails with deep, fair strategy and creator economics that existing games do not offer.  

  

S08. Traction and Metrics Overview  

**Title**  
Traction and metrics overview  

**Status and early signals**  
- Game status: Initial playable build in closed testing on Telegram.  
- Launch plan: First external UA cohorts in [Month Year], focused on [target regions / languages].  
- Goal: Reach Series A-grade retention, monetisation and payback metrics within [X] months of first cohorts.  

**Early signals**

- Waitlist: [X] players have registered interest in Sarge.  
- Internal tests: [N] testers, averaging [T] sessions and [M] minutes per tester per day.  

**Core metrics slot (to fill as data arrives)**  
- MAU: [to be populated after first cohorts].  
- DAU and DAU / MAU: [to be populated after first cohorts].  
- Average sessions and minutes per DAU: [to be populated].  

**Retention targets (Base case)**

- D7: [target percent].  
- D30: [target percent].  
- D60 and D90: [targets] where available.  

**Cohorts and validation pointer**

  - C: Creator-driven UA in [region].  
- All cohorts tracked for retention, ARPDAU, payer rate, CPI and payback; reconciled with the Base case in the financial model.  
- Appendix A2 holds the detailed 30 day validation plan.  

**Honesty note (small text)**

S09. Business Model and Fairness  

**Title**  
Business model and fairness  

**How we make money**  
- Cosmetics and presentation  
  - Skins, effects, banners and other identity items.  
- Battle pass  
  - Seasonal progression with cosmetics, currency and quality-of-life items.  
- Creator events  
  - Optional entry fees for special tournaments; rewards focus on status and expression.  

**Fairness guardrails**  
- No purchasable stats, units, timers or direct power.  
- Matchmaking based on MMR and recency, not spend.  
- Win rate gap between payers and non-payers stays narrow at equivalent MMR.  
- Revenue mix targets  
  - At least 90 percent of revenue from non-power content.  
  - Top one percent of spenders at or below 35 percent of revenue.  

**Why this matters**  
- Players feel they can compete without paying, which supports long-term retention and broad payer participation.  
- Creators can run events without accusations of rigged, pay-to-win outcomes.  

  

S10. Unit Economics and KPI Gates  

**Title**  
Unit economics and KPI gates  

**Target payback profile**  
- Base case  
  - LTV to CPI of at least 3:1 within 180 days.  
  - Retention and ARPDAU tuned for sustainable, profitable UA.  
- Scaling rule  
  - We scale UA only when cohorts meet these payback and retention thresholds.  

**Building LTV (Base case)**  
- Retention  
  - Healthy D1, D7 and D30, with a long tail of engaged strategy players.  
- Monetisation  
  - ARPDAU target band by geo and segment.  
  - Payer rate and ARPPU assumptions grounded in mid-core strategy benchmarks.  
- Cohort model  
  - Cohorts projected monthly; LTV curves and volatility tied directly into our 12 month model.  

**KPI gates**  
- Gate 1: Soft launch viability  
  - D1, D7, D30 retention above minimum thresholds.  
  - CPI in priority channels within target bands.  
- Gate 2: Scale testing  
  - Early payback within 180 days in at least one primary channel.  
  - Stable ARPDAU and payer rate for key cohorts.  
- Gate 3: Pre-Series A check  
  - Consistent LTV to CPI above 3:1 across scaled cohorts.  
  - Sufficient cohort history to support a full Series A raise.  

**Safety valve**  
- UA spend automatically throttles if cohorts fall below gate thresholds, extending runway instead of forcing unprofitable growth.  

  

S11. UA and Growth Engine  

**Title**  
UA and growth engine  

**Channel strategy**  
- Telegram Ads  
  - Primary paid UA, focused on Mini App and bot entry points.  
- Creators and communities  
  - Creator-run tournaments, sponsored events and content as quasi-UA, with revenue sharing.  
- Classic UA (Meta, Google and others)  
  - Tested only once LTV supports them; not a default dependency.  

**Creative and funnel**  
- Creative hooks  
  - “Tap to raid in your chat” moments showing instant, high-stakes fights.  
  - Squad invites, alliance calls to arms and tournament highlights.  
- Funnel design  
  - Deep links into squads, tournaments and replays, not a generic menu.  
  - Clear path to first meaningful win inside the first session.  

**Experiment rhythm and measurement**  
- Weekly  
  - New creative variants per channel.  
  - Cohort performance review and budget reallocation.  
- Monthly  
  - Larger experiments on onboarding, early missions and time to first competitive match.  
- Measurement  
  - All UA tracked at cohort level and reconciled with the financial model and KPI gates in S10.

Appendix A1. Risks and Mitigations  

**Title**  
Risks and mitigations  

Use a table: Risk, Trigger, Mitigation, Owner.  

**Examples**  

Platform risk  
- Trigger  
  - Telegram changes Mini App, bot or payment policies.  
- Mitigation  
  - Maintain close contact with Telegram team.  
  - Build a web client as a controlled fallback.  
  - Monitor policy updates and legal guidance.  
- Owner  
  - CEO.  

UA and CPI risk  
- Trigger  
  - CPIs on Telegram Ads or creator channels materially above modelled bands.  
- Mitigation  
  - Shift mix toward creator tournaments and organic tactics.  
  - Iterate creatives weekly, adjust targeting.  
  - Explore new UA channels once economics support them.  
- Owner  
  - Growth lead.  

Monetisation and fairness risk  
- Trigger  
  - Retention meets targets but ARPDAU lags model, or pressure from players to add pay for power.  
- Mitigation  
  - Expand cosmetic, event and status offerings without breaking fairness rules.  
  - Use surveys and telemetry to tune offers rather than adding power.  
- Owner  
  - Product lead.  

Live ops and content cadence risk  
- Trigger  
  - Elder-game engagement drops; alliances and tournaments lose momentum.  
- Mitigation  
  - Tooling and templates to accelerate event creation.  
  - Clear live ops calendar, with backup events staged.  
- Owner  
  - Live ops producer.  

  

Appendix A2. 30 Day Validation Plan  

**Title**  
30 day validation plan  

**Objectives**  
- Validate that core loop and early game can achieve Base retention targets.  
- Validate CPI bands and early cohort quality on Telegram Ads and creator UA.  
- Collect first monetisation signals without overfitting on early spend.  

**Table: Hypothesis, Metrics, Method, Owner**  

Retention  
- Hypothesis  
  - Improved onboarding and early missions will hit D1/D7/D30 targets.  
- Metrics  
  - D1, D3, D7, D30; tutorial completion; first win rate.  
- Method  
  - A/B onboarding flows and early mission structures across cohorts A, B and C.  
- Owner  
  - Product and design.  

Monetisation  
- Hypothesis  
  - A light cosmetic store plus early battle pass preview will generate healthy ARPDAU without harming fairness perception.  
- Metrics  
  - ARPDAU, payer rate, early ARPPU, fairness NPS or survey scores.  
- Method  
  - Limited-time test store and pass; follow up with surveys and cohort analysis.  
- Owner  
  - Product and data.  

UA and CPI  
- Hypothesis  
  - Telegram Ads and creator posts can reach CPI bands assumed in the Base case.  
- Metrics  
  - CPI by channel, click-through rate, install-to-battle conversion.  
- Method  
  - Small, capped UA experiments per channel; adjust targeting and creatives weekly.  
- Owner  
  - Growth and UA.  

**Success bars and actions**  
- If bars are hit  
  - Expand cohorts, broaden creative testing, move toward Gate 2.  
- If bars are missed  
  - Prioritise product and funnel changes over spend, re-test before scaling UA.

    print(slide_names[:10])

[ASSISTANT]
import xml.etree.ElementTree as ET

def extract_text_from_slide(xml_bytes):
    root = ET.fromstring(xml_bytes)
    # pptx slide text in a:t elements
    texts = []
    for elem in root.iter():
        if elem.tag.endswith('}t'):
            if elem.text:
                texts.append(elem.text)
    return "\n".join(texts)

slides_text = []
with ZipFile(pptx_path) as z:
    for name in slide_names:
        xml_bytes = z.read(name)
        text = extract_text_from_slide(xml_bytes)
        slides_text.append((name, text))

S01. Cover and Stage  

**Title**  
SARGE UNIVERSE  
The strategy game on Telegram  

**Subtitle**  
Deep, fair war inside your Telegram chats  

**Stage label**  
Stage: Initial playable in closed testing. Raising to reach Series A metrics on Telegram.  

**Tagline bullets**  
- One-tap from chat into real battles, no app store friction  
- Build bases, fight real opponents and join creator-run tournaments without leaving Telegram  

**Footer**  
- Studio logo  
- Contact: [Name], [Role], [Email], [Telegram handle]  

`---`

S02. Vision  

**Title**  
Vision  

**Body**  

Sarge Universe is a deep, session-friendly war game that runs entirely inside Telegram. Players drop from links in chats straight into real battles, then grow persistent armies and bases over time.  

Today we have a working core loop on Telegram, an initial playable build and a disciplined plan to validate retention, monetisation and UA specifically on this platform.  

At scale, Sarge becomes the reference strategy franchise on Telegram: fair by design, creator powered, and capable of supporting millions of daily players and tournaments across regions.  

`---`

S03. Player Problem and Fantasy  

**Title**  
Player problem and fantasy  

**Problem**  
- Telegram is full of clickers, tap games and meme titles, but almost no true mid-core strategy with depth, fairness and long-term progression.  
- Strategy players bounce between heavy mobile 4X and PC, with friction between their social graph and the games they play.  
- Creators on Telegram have large, engaged audiences but no deep, integrated game experiences they can own and monetise.  

**Fantasy Sarge delivers**  
- “My army lives in my chats.” I tap a link and I am in a real fight within seconds.  
- I play in tournaments run by creators and community leaders I already follow, with visible ladders and real bragging rights.  
- I trust that money buys expression and convenience, not raw power, so I can compete without paying and still feel respected.  

**Future proof point**  
- Slot for 2–3 real player quotes from early cohorts or community once tests are live.  

`---`

S04. Product Today – Status and Scope  

**Title**  
Product today  

**Current playable**  
- Core loop from Telegram link into first battle is implemented and stable.  
- Base building, army composition and PvP skirmishes are functional in the current build.  
- We can already exercise progression, fairness rules and early elder-game dynamics.  

**Systems in place**  
- Progression spine  
  - Resources, upgrades and tech tree up to elder-game tiers.  
  - Multiple unit families and counters already implemented.  
- Fairness enforcement  
  - Matchmaking uses MMR and recency only.  
  - No purchasable stats, units, timers or power in the live design.  
- Tournament framework  
  - Creator-run tournament scaffolding integrated into the game: brackets, rewards, rulesets and one-tap join from chat.  

**Next steps before soft launch**  
- Tighten onboarding and early campaign to teach depth without overwhelming new players.  
- Layer first live events and seasonal structure on top of the loop.  
- Wire full telemetry and cohort tracking into the financial model and UA tooling.  

`---`

S05. Core Loop and Progression  

**Title**  
Core loop and progression  

**Core loop**  
- Tap a Sarge link in a chat or channel.  
- Drop into a real-time battle in under five seconds.  
- Earn resources and rewards based on performance.  
- Invest into base upgrades, tech tree and army composition.  
- Unlock new units, abilities and tournament brackets.  

**Progression arc**  
- Early game  
  - Learn basic commands, unlock first units and upgrades.  
  - Short, snackable sessions that fit Telegram usage.  
- Mid game  
  - Deeper tech choices, army specialisation and real counter-play.  
  - Alliances and social structures begin to matter.  
- Elder game  
  - Territory control, alliance coordination and long-running wars.  
  - High-stakes creator-run tournaments with unique formats and rewards.  

**Design goals**  
- Multiple viable strategies, not a single dominant meta.  
- Battles that are readable in seconds yet reward deep mastery.  
- Progression tuned for years of play, not months.  

`---`

S06. Market and Why Telegram  

**Title**  
Market and why Telegram  

**Telegram as a platform**  
- Around 900M monthly active users, with Mini Apps that run inside chat threads.  
- Mini Apps remove install friction entirely: players go from curiosity to combat in seconds, not minutes or hours.  
- Virality is native: forwards, channels, groups and bot commands drive organic discovery.  

**Strategy gap on Telegram**  
- Current top Telegram games skew towards tap-to-earn, clickers and simple farming loops.  
- Mid-core strategy depth is dramatically under-served on Telegram compared to the size of the user base.  
- Players and creators who enjoy strategy content have nowhere on-platform to play a serious war game.  

**Size of the wedge**  
- Telegram user base: about 900M.  
- Telegram gamers: around 200M.  
- Telegram PvP players: roughly 120M.  
- In our funnel model, acquiring 25 percent of PvP players over time implies about 30M Sarge players. We need only a fraction of that as DAU to support a very large game.  

**Proof points**  
- Notcoin reported roughly 40M players by June 2024.  
- Hamster Kombat claimed around 300M users by August 2024.  
- These show how quickly native Telegram games can scale when they match the platform.  

**What Sarge unlocks**  
- A flagship strategy franchise designed specifically for Telegram’s rails.  
- A new class of creator-owned tournaments and events inside chat.  

`---`

S07. Competition and Position  

**Title**  
Competition and position  

**Depth and skill**  
- Sarge Universe  
  - Full army building, tech choices and elder game; multiple viable metas.  
- Telegram tap games  
  - Light progression and simple loops; often a single “best way” to play.  
- Classic mobile 4X or war  
  - Deep but grind-heavy, with chores and time gates.  

**Friction and access**  
- Sarge Universe  
  - Open from a link in chat, straight into battle, no install.  
- Telegram tap games  
  - Also instant, but built around thin mechanics and shallow loops.  
- Classic 4X or war  
  - App store page, download, install and long tutorial before the first meaningful fight.  

**Fairness and monetisation**  
- Sarge Universe  
  - No purchasable stats, units, timers or power.  
  - Cosmetics-first monetisation with explicit guardrails.  
- Telegram tap games  
  - Often ad driven or tap-to-earn, with minimal fairness considerations.  
- Classic 4X or war  
  - Heavy use of time skips, purchasable power and aggressive VIP ladders.  

**Social and creator upside**  
- Sarge Universe  
  - Creator-run tournaments with revenue sharing and in-game tools for hosting events.  
- Telegram tap games  
  - Limited creator integration, mostly referral codes and basic promotions.  
- Classic 4X or war  
  - Creators operate outside the game via streams and content, not as in-game organisers.  

**Positioning sentence**  
- Sarge combines Telegram’s instant rails with deep, fair strategy and creator economics that existing games do not offer.

`---`

S09. Business Model and Fairness  

**Title**  
Business model and fairness  

**How we make money**  
- Cosmetics and presentation  
  - Skins, effects, banners and other identity items.  
- Battle Pass  
  - Seasonal progression with cosmetics, currency and quality of life items.  
- Creator events  
  - Optional entry fees for special tournaments; rewards focus on status and expression, not power.  

**Fairness guardrails**  
- Cosmetics first monetisation. No pay for power.  
- Progression power is earned by play.  
- No purchasable stats, units, timers or power.  
- Store features cosmetics, effects, skins and UI themes only.  

**Fairness guardrails for soft launch**  
- Matchmaking uses MMR and recency only.  
- Payer vs non payer win rate gap under 1 percentage point at matched MMR.  
- Correlation of spend to win rate within MMR bands r ≤ 0.05.  
- Revenue mix at least 90 percent from non power cosmetics.  
- Top 1 percent spenders at or below 35 percent of revenue.  

**Retention link**  
- Quests, limited time events and creator tournaments create reasons to return and spend on identity and celebration.  

`---`

S10. Unit Economics and KPI Gates  

**Title**  
Unit economics and KPI gates  

**Target payback profile**  
- Base case  
  - LTV to CPI of at least 3:1 within 180 days.  
  - Retention and ARPDAU tuned for sustainable, profitable UA.  
- Scaling rule  
  - We scale UA only when cohorts meet these payback and retention thresholds.  

**Forecast bands from current model**  
- LTV Day 30 forecast: about £0.63 in our Base case.  
- Example payback sensitivity band, based on the “Unit Economics Snapshot” slide:  
  - CPI low (£0.10): payback roughly 3 days.  
  - CPI mid (£0.20): payback roughly 8 days.  
  - CPI high (£0.50): payback roughly 23 days.  
- Geography: global blended.  
- Channel: Telegram Ads.  
- Creative: tournament invitation format.  
- Clearly labelled as forecast until validated by cohort data from soft launch testing.  

**Building LTV (Base case)**  
- Retention  
  - Uses the Base D1, D7, D30 targets shown in S08 and extended to 180 days for LTV.  
- Monetisation  
  - ARPDAU target bands and payer rate / ARPPU assumptions grounded in mid-core strategy benchmarks.  
- Cohort model  
  - Cohorts projected monthly; LTV curves and volatility tied directly into the 12 month model.  

**KPI gates**  
- Gate 1: Soft launch viability  
  - Tap to first session, D1, D7, D30 retention above minimum thresholds.  
  - CPI by geo and channel within target bands (roughly £0.10 to £0.50 depending on mix).  
- Gate 2: Scale testing  
  - Early payback within 180 days in at least one primary channel.  
  - Stable ARPDAU and payer rate for key cohorts.  
- Gate 3: Pre Series A check  
  - Consistent LTV to CPI above 3:1 across scaled cohorts.  
  - Enough cohort history to support a full Series A raise.  

**Safety valve**  
- UA spend throttles automatically if cohorts fall below gate thresholds, extending runway instead of forcing unprofitable growth.

**Channel strategy**

**Experiment rhythm and Gate 1 discipline**  
- Based on the “Go-to-Market: Flywheel and Gate 1” slide:  
  - Weeks −8 to −5: trailer, hero art batch, around 300 player closed alpha.  
  - Weeks −4 to −1: community build and press activation, whitelist microsite, Discord live, KPI such as 10,000 whitelist sign ups on a small budget.  
  - Launch week: public release with creator led tournament and replay share hooks.  
  - Weeks 1 to 4: onboarding, monetisation and engagement experiments, weekly creative tests and bracket events.  
  - Weeks 5 to 12: expansion, creator tools and event cadence.  
- Gate 1 discipline  
  - Scale UA only when D7 retention is at or above 19 percent and CPI stays within target bands.  

**Measurement**

- Owner  
  - CEO.

  - Iterate creatives weekly, adjust targeting.  
  - Explore new UA channels once economics support them.  
- Owner  
  - Growth lead.  

**Monetisation and fairness risk**

  - Retention meets targets but ARPDAU lags model, or pressure from players to add pay for power.  
- Mitigation  
  - Expand cosmetic, event and status offerings without breaking fairness rules.  
  - Use surveys and telemetry to tune offers rather than adding power.  
- Owner  
  - Product lead.  

**Live ops and content cadence risk**  
- Trigger  
  - Elder game engagement drops; alliances and tournaments lose momentum.  
- Mitigation  
  - Tooling and templates to accelerate event creation.  
  - Clear live ops calendar, with backup events staged.  
- Owner  
  - Live ops producer.

and you think this is an award winning pitch slide deck?

[ASSISTANT]
No.  

For a true “award winning” Series A pitch deck, this is not there yet. It is structurally much better than where we started, but it falls short on four big axes that your own guide cares about: evidence, sharpness, distinctiveness and integration with the existing material.

I will be blunt and specific.

### 1. Stage vs Series A expectations

Against the guide’s Series A bar:

- You have zero live cohort data in the deck.  
- No MAU, DAU, DAU / MAU, retention curves, ARPDAU, CPI or payback numbers.  
- All economics are modelled, not observed.

That means:

- At *best* this is a “Series A shaped, late Seed / pre–Series A” deck.  
- It *cannot* be a 10 of 10 Series A deck, because the guide defines Series A quality primarily through real KPIs and learnings.

Best you can do right now is “10 of 10 for a pre-launch deck that is honest about stage.” We are not yet at that either, for the reasons below.

### 2. Text density and investor readability

What we produced:

- Many slides read like tightened Word pages, not true investor slides.  
- Bullet stacks are long, and several slides carry three or four sections of text.

Where this conflicts with the guide:

- The guide expects that by Series A, story slides are *shorter*, not longer, because investors spend time on metrics and economics.  
- An award level deck would push most of the explanatory paragraphs into speaker notes or appendix, and keep visible content highly scannable.

Concrete examples:

- S03, S04, S05 and S06 are all word heavy.  
- S08 has three different conceptual blocks before you ever see a metric.  
- A judge or partner flicking through will skim or skip.

So even for your current stage, this is more like a 6 to 7 out of 10 on craft and compression.

### 3. Distinctiveness and “only Sarge could say this”

A 10 of 10 deck for Sarge on Telegram should have:

- At least three to five very specific Sarge insights about Telegram, Notcoin, Hamster, creator economics and how strategy behaves on this platform.  
- Concrete examples of the kind of tournaments, alliances and social behaviours you are designing for.  
- A feeling of “this team has thought more deeply about Telegram strategy than anyone else.”

Our draft:

- Uses good generic language on Telegram and strategy, but very little that is uniquely Sarge.  
- Competition slide is solid structurally, but could apply to almost any mid-core Telegram strategy pitch.  
- UA and growth slide is mostly textbook rather than war stories or sharp hypotheses tied to your GTM slide in the PPT.

We pulled concepts from the existing deck and chats (fairness rules, funnel, Gate 1, model sensitivities), but we did not lean hard enough into the specific, quirky advantages of Sarge and your thinking.

So distinctiveness is maybe 6 of 10. An award level deck needs to feel like 9 or 10 on “this is clearly *their* game and *their* edge.”

### 4. Evidence and integration with the existing deck

You asked us to use:

- The attached “Sarge Universe – THE Strategy Game on Telegram.pptx”.  
- The investor pitch guide.  
- Sarge Universe project chats.

What we did:

- Used the PPT conceptually: the 5-second loop, Gate 1 timeline, unit economics snapshot, fairness rules and UA plan all show up in the content.  
- Used the guide structurally: 14 core slides, with a metrics hub, economics slide, UA slide, org slide and plan slide.

What we did not do well enough:

- We did not systematically lift *exact* numbers and phrasings where you already had them validated in the PPT.  
- The “forecast bands” on unit economics and some of the GTM detail are still loosely anchored rather than clearly mapped cell by cell to your current snapshot slide.  
- We did not pull through specific examples and visuals from the current deck (for example, the Gate 1 diagram, the funnel graphic) into the written structure.

That undermines:

- Consistency with your existing work.  
- Your ability to trust that if you paste this into PowerPoint, it will perfectly line up with the existing unit economics and GTM slides.

So on integration with the PPT and guide, this is maybe a 7 of 10.

### 5. Story arc and emotional hook

What works:

- The basic arc is right: What Sarge is, why Telegram, why now, what we have built, why it is fair, how we scale and how we spend.  
- Fairness and creator tournaments are good thematic through-lines.

What is missing for 10 of 10:

- A sharper opening hook. One killer visual or one sentence that makes people sit up, built around a hard number or a very concrete Telegram story.  
- More use of examples: “Here is what a real creator tournament looks like in Sarge,” “Here is what a replay share looks like in chat,” etc.  
- A stronger “why us” section that draws on your actual background and pattern recognition with past titles.

Our current text deck has the bones, but it still feels like a well organised draft, not a weaponised narrative.

### 6. Mechanical issues and your constraints

You also asked for:

- No horizontal rules and no em dashes. I already broke that earlier in the process by using `---` as separators before you corrected it. That is on me.  
- Strict alignment to your guide’s slide count and roles. We are close on that, but not perfectly tuned, especially where the guide expects true Series A metrics.

So there are still mechanical deviations from your operating rules, which by definition keeps this away from “award winning” in your own internal standard.

### Realistic framing

Given your current stage:

- You are pre soft launch, with no live KPIs.  
- Your own guide uses Series A to mean “several months of live data, tested UA, clear LTV to CPI proof.”

So:

- A *true* 10 of 10 Series A deck is impossible until you have cohorts and revenue.  
- The target we should be aiming for now is:  
  “10 of 10 *pre-launch* deck that cleanly sets up a data room and raises money to hit Series A metrics.”

We are not yet at that target. This is closer to a 6 to 7 out of 10 against that bar.

### What to do next if you want to push this toward that 10 of 10 (for your stage)

If you want to continue, the path I would suggest, one step at a time, is:

1. Pick one cluster to truly bring to 9 or 10 out of 10, instead of trying to perfect everything at once.  
   For example: the “Why Telegram / Market / Competition” cluster (S06 and S07), or the “Fairness and Business Model” cluster (S09 and S10).

2. For that cluster, we:  
   - Cross check every number and phrase against the current PPT.  
   - Strip 30 to 40 percent of the words while keeping content.  
   - Add at least one concrete example, graphic concept, or number that only Sarge could say.  

3. Then move to the next cluster.

If you want, tell me which cluster you want to push first and I will stop critiquing and start rebuilding *just those two or three slides* to a standard that you feel is genuinely “this could stand in front of a top tier fund as the spine of the discussion.”

def get_slide_text(slide):
    texts = []
    for shape in slide.shapes:
        if hasattr(shape, "text") and shape.text.strip():
            texts.append(shape.text)
    return "\n".join(texts)
